	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Imperialism</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>
		
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Policy or ideology of extending a nation's rule over foreign nations</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Imperialism_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Imperialism (disambiguation)">Imperialism (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Punch_Rhodes_Colossus.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Punch_Rhodes_Colossus.png/220px-Punch_Rhodes_Colossus.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="285" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Punch_Rhodes_Colossus.png/330px-Punch_Rhodes_Colossus.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Punch_Rhodes_Colossus.png/440px-Punch_Rhodes_Colossus.png 2x" data-file-width="2069" data-file-height="2681" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Punch_Rhodes_Colossus.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Cecil_Rhodes" title="Cecil Rhodes">Cecil Rhodes</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Cape-Cairo_railway" class="mw-redirect" title="Cape-Cairo railway">Cape-Cairo railway</a> project. Rhodes founded the <a href="/wiki/De_Beers_Mining_Company" class="mw-redirect" title="De Beers Mining Company">De Beers Mining Company</a>, owned the <a href="/wiki/British_South_Africa_Company" title="British South Africa Company">British South Africa Company</a> and had his name given to what became the state of <a href="/wiki/Rhodesia" title="Rhodesia">Rhodesia</a>. He liked to "paint the map <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British</a> red" and declared: "all of these stars&#160;... these vast worlds that remain out of reach. If I could, I would annex other planets".<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>

<p><b>Imperialism</b> is a policy or ideology of extending a country's rule over foreign nations, often by military force or by gaining political and economic control of other areas.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Imperialism was both normal and common <i>worldwide</i> <a href="/wiki/List_of_largest_empires" title="List of largest empires">throughout recorded history</a>, the earliest examples dating from the mid-third millennium BC. In recent times, it has been considered morally reprehensible and <a href="/wiki/Self-determination" title="Self-determination">prohibited by international law</a>. Therefore, the term is used in international propaganda to denounce an opponent's foreign policy.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The term can be applied  to the colonization of the Americas between the 17th and 19th centuries, as opposed to <a href="/wiki/New_Imperialism" title="New Imperialism">New Imperialism</a>, which describes the expansion of <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">Western Powers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Empire_of_Japan" title="Empire of Japan">Japan</a> during the late 19th and early 20th centuries. However, both are examples of imperialism.
</p>

<ul>

</li>

</div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology_and_usage">Etymology and usage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Imperialism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology and usage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The word imperialism originated from the Latin word <i><a href="/wiki/Imperium" title="Imperium">imperium</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> which means supreme power. It first became common with its current sense in <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Great Britain</a>, during the 1870s and was used with a negative connotation.<sup id="cite_ref-Magnusson_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Magnusson-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Previously the word imperialism had been used to describe to what was perceived as <a href="/wiki/Napoleon_III" title="Napoleon III">Napoleon III</a>'s attempts of obtaining political support through foreign military interventions.<sup id="cite_ref-Magnusson_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Magnusson-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> The term was and is mainly applied to Western (and Japanese) political and economic dominance especially in Asia and Africa, in the 19th and 20th centuries. Its precise meaning continues to be debated by scholars. Some writers, such as <a href="/wiki/Edward_Said" title="Edward Said">Edward Said</a>, use the term more broadly to describe any system of domination and subordination organised with an imperial center and a periphery.<sup id="cite_ref-Edward_Said_1994._P._9_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Edward_Said_1994._P._9-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> This definition encompasses both nominal <a href="/wiki/Empire" title="Empire">empires</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neocolonialism" title="Neocolonialism">neocolonialism</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Colonialism_versus_imperialism">Colonialism versus imperialism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Imperialism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Colonialism versus imperialism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:442px;"><a href="/wiki/File:World_1898_empires_colonies_territory.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/World_1898_empires_colonies_territory.png/440px-World_1898_empires_colonies_territory.png" decoding="async" width="440" height="176" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/World_1898_empires_colonies_territory.png/660px-World_1898_empires_colonies_territory.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/World_1898_empires_colonies_territory.png/880px-World_1898_empires_colonies_territory.png 2x" data-file-width="1560" data-file-height="624" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:World_1898_empires_colonies_territory.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Imperial powers in 1898</div></div></div>
<p>"The word 'empire' comes from the Latin word imperium; for which the closest modern English equivalent would perhaps be '<a href="/wiki/Sovereignty" title="Sovereignty">sovereignty</a>', or simply 'rule'".<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> The greatest distinction of an empire is through the amount of land that a nation has conquered and expanded. Political power grows from conquering land; however, cultural and economic aspects flourish through sea and trade routes.  A distinction about empires is "that although political empires were built mostly by expansion overland, economic and cultural influences spread at least as much by sea".<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Some of the main aspects of trade that went overseas consisted of animals and plant products. European empires in Asia and Africa "have come to be seen as the classic forms of imperialism: and indeed most books on the subject confine themselves to the European seaborne empires".<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> European expansion caused the world to be divided by how developed and developing nation are portrayed through the <a href="/wiki/World_systems_theory" class="mw-redirect" title="World systems theory">world systems theory</a>. The two main regions are the core and the periphery. The core consists of areas of high income and profit; the periphery is on the opposing side of the spectrum consisting of areas of low income and profit. These critical theories of <a href="/wiki/Geopolitics" title="Geopolitics">geo-politics</a> have led to increased discussion of the meaning and impact of imperialism on the modern post-colonial world. The Russian leader Lenin suggested that "imperialism was the highest form of capitalism, claiming that imperialism developed after colonialism, and was distinguished from colonialism by monopoly capitalism".<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> This idea from Lenin stresses how important new political world order has become in the modern era. Geopolitics now focuses on states becoming major economic players in the market; some states today are viewed as empires due to their political and economic authority over other nations.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Roubaud._Russian_troops_entering_Tiflis_in_1799.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Roubaud._Russian_troops_entering_Tiflis_in_1799.JPG/220px-Roubaud._Russian_troops_entering_Tiflis_in_1799.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="139" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Roubaud._Russian_troops_entering_Tiflis_in_1799.JPG/330px-Roubaud._Russian_troops_entering_Tiflis_in_1799.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Roubaud._Russian_troops_entering_Tiflis_in_1799.JPG/440px-Roubaud._Russian_troops_entering_Tiflis_in_1799.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1084" data-file-height="683" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Roubaud._Russian_troops_entering_Tiflis_in_1799.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Entrance of the Russian troops in Tiflis, 26 November 1799</i>, by <a href="/wiki/Franz_Roubaud" title="Franz Roubaud">Franz Roubaud</a>, 1886</div></div></div>
<p>The term "imperialism" is often conflated with "<a href="/wiki/Colonialism" title="Colonialism">colonialism</a>"; however, many scholars have argued that each have their own distinct definition. Imperialism and colonialism have been used in order to describe one's perceived superiority, domination and influence upon a person or group of people. <a href="/wiki/Robert_J._C._Young" title="Robert J. C. Young">Robert Young</a> writes that while imperialism operates from the center, is a state policy and is developed for ideological as well as financial reasons, colonialism is simply the development for settlement or commercial intentions. However, colonialism still includes invasion.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Colonialism in modern usage also tends to imply a degree of geographic separation between the colony and the imperial power. Particularly, Edward Said distinguishes the difference between imperialism and colonialism by stating; "imperialism involved 'the practice, the theory and the attitudes of a dominating metropolitan center ruling a distant territory', while colonialism refers to the 'implanting of settlements on a distant territory.'<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Contiguous land empires such as the Russian or Ottoman have traditionally been excluded from discussions of colonialism, though this is beginning to change, since it is accepted that they also sent populations into the territories they ruled.<sup id="cite_ref-Gallaher_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gallaher-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>116</span></sup>
</p><p>Imperialism and colonialism both dictate the political and economic advantage over a land and the indigenous populations they control, yet scholars sometimes find it difficult to illustrate the difference between the two.<sup id="cite_ref-Painter_&amp;_Jeffrey_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Painter_&amp;_Jeffrey-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>107</span></sup> Although imperialism and colonialism focus on the suppression of <i>an other</i>, if colonialism refers to the process of a country taking physical control of another, imperialism refers to the political and monetary dominance, either formally or informally. Colonialism is seen to be the architect deciding how to start dominating areas and then imperialism can be seen as creating the idea behind conquest cooperating with colonialism. Colonialism is when the imperial nation begins a conquest over an area and then eventually is able to rule over the areas the previous nation had controlled. Colonialism's core meaning is the exploitation of the valuable assets and supplies of the nation that was conquered and the conquering nation then gaining the benefits from the spoils of the war.<sup id="cite_ref-Painter_&amp;_Jeffrey_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Painter_&amp;_Jeffrey-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>170–75</span></sup> The meaning of imperialism is to create an empire, by conquering the other state's lands and therefore increasing its own dominance. Colonialism is the builder and preserver of the colonial possessions in an area by a population coming from a foreign region.<sup id="cite_ref-Painter_&amp;_Jeffrey_14-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Painter_&amp;_Jeffrey-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>173–76</span></sup> Colonialism can completely change the existing social structure, physical structure and economics of an area; it is not unusual that the characteristics of the conquering peoples are inherited by the conquered indigenous populations.<sup id="cite_ref-Painter_&amp;_Jeffrey_14-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Painter_&amp;_Jeffrey-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>41</span></sup> Few colonies remain remote from their mother country. Thus, most will eventually establish a separate nationality or remain under complete control of their mother colony.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Justification">Justification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Imperialism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Justification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:China_imperialism_cartoon.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/China_imperialism_cartoon.jpg/170px-China_imperialism_cartoon.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="233" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/China_imperialism_cartoon.jpg/255px-China_imperialism_cartoon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/China_imperialism_cartoon.jpg/340px-China_imperialism_cartoon.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3118" data-file-height="4267" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:China_imperialism_cartoon.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A shocked <a href="/wiki/Mandarin_(bureaucrat)" title="Mandarin (bureaucrat)">mandarin</a> in <a href="/wiki/Manchu_people" title="Manchu people">Manchu</a> robe in the back, with <a href="/wiki/Queen_Victoria" title="Queen Victoria">Queen Victoria</a> (<a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a>), <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_II_of_Germany" class="mw-redirect" title="Wilhelm II of Germany">Wilhelm II</a> (<a href="/wiki/German_Empire" title="German Empire">German Empire</a>), <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_II_of_Russia" title="Nicholas II of Russia">Nicholas II</a> (<a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Imperial Russia</a>), <a href="/wiki/Marianne" title="Marianne">Marianne</a> (<a href="/wiki/French_Third_Republic" title="French Third Republic">French Third Republic</a>), and a <a href="/wiki/Samurai" title="Samurai">samurai</a> (<a href="/wiki/Empire_of_Japan" title="Empire of Japan">Empire of Japan</a>) stabbing into a <a href="/wiki/King_cake" title="King cake">king cake</a> with <i>Chine</i> ("China" in French) written on it. A portrayal of New Imperialism and its effects on China.</div></div></div>
<p>Stephen Howe has summarized his view on the beneficial effects of the colonial empires:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047036">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>At least some of the great modern empires&#160;–  the British, French, Austro-Hungarian, Russian, and even the Ottoman&#160;– have virtues that have been too readily forgotten. They provided stability, security, and legal order for their subjects.  They constrained, and at their best, tried to transcend, the potentially savage ethnic or religious antagonisms among the peoples. And the aristocracies which ruled most of them were often far more liberal, humane, and cosmopolitan than their supposedly ever more democratic successors.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>A controversial aspect of imperialism is the defense and justification of empire-building based on seemingly rational grounds. In <a href="/wiki/Ancient_China" class="mw-redirect" title="Ancient China">ancient China</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tianxia" title="Tianxia">Tianxia</a> denoted the lands, space, and area divinely appointed to the <a href="/wiki/Emperor" title="Emperor">Emperor</a> by universal and well-defined principles of order. The center of this land was directly apportioned to the Imperial court, forming the center of a world view that centered on the Imperial court and went concentrically outward to major and minor officials and then the common citizens, <a href="/wiki/Tributary_states" class="mw-redirect" title="Tributary states">tributary states</a>, and finally ending with the fringe "<a href="/wiki/Barbarians" class="mw-redirect" title="Barbarians">barbarians</a>". Tianxia's idea of hierarchy gave <a href="/wiki/Chinese_people" title="Chinese people">Chinese</a> a privileged position and was justified through the promise of order and peace. <a href="/wiki/J._A._Hobson" title="J. A. Hobson">J. A. Hobson</a> identifies this justification on general grounds as: "It is desirable that the earth should be peopled, governed, and developed, as far as possible, by the races which can do this work best, i.e. by the races of highest 'social efficiency'".<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>  Many others argued that imperialism is justified for several different reasons. <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Ratzel" title="Friedrich Ratzel">Friedrich Ratzel</a> believed that in order for a state to survive, imperialism was needed. <a href="/wiki/Halford_Mackinder" title="Halford Mackinder">Halford Mackinder</a> felt that <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Great Britain</a> needed to be one of the greatest imperialists and therefore justified imperialism.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> The purportedly scientific nature of "<a href="/wiki/Social_Darwinism" title="Social Darwinism">Social Darwinism</a>" and a theory of races formed a supposedly rational justification for imperialism. Under this doctrine, the French politician Jules Ferry could declare in 1883 that "Superior races have a right, because they have a duty. They have the duty to civilize the inferior races."<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> The rhetoric of colonizers being racially superior appears to have achieved its purpose, for example throughout Latin America "whiteness" is still prized today and various forms of <a href="/wiki/Blanqueamiento" title="Blanqueamiento">blanqueamiento</a> (whitening) are common.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Royal_Geographical_Society" title="Royal Geographical Society">Royal Geographical Society of London</a> and other geographical societies in Europe had great influence and were able to fund travelers who would come back with tales of their discoveries.<sup id="cite_ref-Gilmartin_2009_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gilmartin_2009-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> These societies also served as a space for travellers to share these stories.<sup id="cite_ref-Gilmartin_2009_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gilmartin_2009-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Political geographers such as Friedrich Ratzel of Germany and Halford Mackinder of Britain also supported imperialism.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Ratzel believed expansion was necessary for a state's survival while Mackinder supported Britain's imperial expansion; these two arguments dominated the discipline for decades.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Geographical theories such as <a href="/wiki/Environmental_determinism" title="Environmental determinism">environmental determinism</a> also suggested that tropical environments created uncivilized people in need of European guidance.<sup id="cite_ref-Gilmartin_2009_21-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gilmartin_2009-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> For instance, American geographer <a href="/wiki/Ellen_Churchill_Semple" title="Ellen Churchill Semple">Ellen Churchill Semple</a> argued that even though human beings originated in the <a href="/wiki/Tropics" title="Tropics">tropics</a> they were only able to become fully human in the <a href="/wiki/Temperate_climate" title="Temperate climate">temperate</a> zone.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Tropicality can be paralleled with Edward Said's <a href="/wiki/Orientalism" title="Orientalism">Orientalism</a> as the west's construction of the east as the "other".<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> According to Said, orientalism allowed Europe to establish itself as the superior and the norm, which justified its dominance over the essentialized Orient.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Technology" title="Technology">Technology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Economic_efficiency" title="Economic efficiency">economic efficiency</a> were often improved in territories subjected to imperialism through the building of roads, other infrastructure and introduction of new technologies.
</p><p>The principles of imperialism are often generalizable to the policies and practices of the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a> "during the last generation, and proceeds rather by diagnosis than by historical description".<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> British imperialism in some sparsely-inhabited regions appears to have applied a principle now termed <a href="/wiki/Terra_nullius" title="Terra nullius">Terra nullius</a> (Latin expression which stems from <a href="/wiki/Roman_law" title="Roman law">Roman law</a> meaning 'no man's land'). The country of <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a> serves as a case study in relation to British settlement and colonial rule of the continent in the 18th century, that was arguably premised on <i>terra nullius</i>, as its settlers considered it unused by its original inhabitants.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Orientalism_and_imaginative_geography">Orientalism and imaginative geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Imperialism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Orientalism and imaginative geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Imperial control, territorial and <a href="/wiki/Cultural_imperialism" title="Cultural imperialism">cultural</a>, is justified through <a href="/wiki/Discourse" title="Discourse">discourses</a> about the imperialists' understanding of different spaces.<sup id="cite_ref-Hubbard,_P._2010_p._239_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hubbard,_P._2010_p._239-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Conceptually, <a href="/wiki/Imagined_geographies" title="Imagined geographies">imagined geographies</a> explain the limitations of the imperialist understanding of the societies (human reality) of the different spaces inhabited by the non–European Other.<sup id="cite_ref-Hubbard,_P._2010_p._239_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hubbard,_P._2010_p._239-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In <i><a href="/wiki/Orientalism_(book)" title="Orientalism (book)">Orientalism</a></i> (1978), <a href="/wiki/Edward_Said" title="Edward Said">Edward Said</a> said that the West developed the concept of <a href="/wiki/The_Orient" class="mw-redirect" title="The Orient">The Orient</a>—an imagined geography of the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_world" title="Eastern world">Eastern world</a>—which functions as an <a href="/wiki/Essentialism" title="Essentialism">essentializing</a> discourse that represents neither the ethnic diversity nor the social reality of the Eastern world.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> That by reducing the East into cultural essences, the imperial discourse uses place-based identities to create <a href="/wiki/Difference_(philosophy)" title="Difference (philosophy)">cultural difference</a> and psychologic distance between "We, the West" and "They, the East" and between "Here, in the West" and "There, in the East".<sup id="cite_ref-Said,_Edward_1979_p.357_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Said,_Edward_1979_p.357-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>That cultural differentiation was especially noticeable in the books and paintings of early <a href="/wiki/Oriental_studies" title="Oriental studies">Oriental studies</a>, the European examinations of the Orient, which misrepresented the East as irrational and backward, the opposite of the rational and progressive West.<sup id="cite_ref-Hubbard,_P._2010_p._239_28-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hubbard,_P._2010_p._239-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Defining the East as a negative vision of the Western world, as its inferior, not only increased the sense-of-self of the West, but also was a way of ordering the East, and making it known to the West, so that it could be dominated and controlled.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Therefore, Orientalism was the ideological justification of early Western imperialism—a body of knowledge and ideas that rationalized social, cultural, political, and economic control of other, non-white peoples.<sup id="cite_ref-Said,_Edward_1979_p.357_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Said,_Edward_1979_p.357-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cartography">Cartography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Imperialism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Cartography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Cartographic_propaganda" title="Cartographic propaganda">Cartographic propaganda</a></div>
<p>One of the main tools used by imperialists was cartography. <a href="/wiki/Cartography" title="Cartography">Cartography</a> is "the art, science and technology of making maps"<sup id="cite_ref-Harley,_J.B._1989_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harley,_J.B._1989-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> but this definition is problematic. It implies that maps are objective representations of the world when in reality they serve very political means.<sup id="cite_ref-Harley,_J.B._1989_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harley,_J.B._1989-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> For Harley, maps serve as an example of <a href="/wiki/Michel_Foucault" title="Michel Foucault">Foucault's</a> <a href="/wiki/Power_(social_and_political)" title="Power (social and political)">power</a> and <a href="/wiki/Knowledge" title="Knowledge">knowledge</a> concept.
</p><p>To better illustrate this idea, Bassett focuses his analysis of the role of 19th-century maps during the "<a href="/wiki/Scramble_for_Africa" title="Scramble for Africa">scramble for Africa</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-Bassett,_Thomas_J._1994_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bassett,_Thomas_J._1994-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> He states that maps "contributed to empire by promoting, assisting, and legitimizing the extension of French and British power into West Africa".<sup id="cite_ref-Bassett,_Thomas_J._1994_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bassett,_Thomas_J._1994-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> During his analysis of 19th-century cartographic techniques, he highlights the use of blank space to denote unknown or unexplored territory.<sup id="cite_ref-Bassett,_Thomas_J._1994_36-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bassett,_Thomas_J._1994-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> This provided incentives for imperial and colonial powers to obtain "information to fill in blank spaces on contemporary maps".<sup id="cite_ref-Bassett,_Thomas_J._1994_36-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bassett,_Thomas_J._1994-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Although cartographic processes advanced through imperialism, further analysis of their progress reveals many biases linked to <a href="/wiki/Eurocentrism" title="Eurocentrism">eurocentrism</a>. According to Bassett, "[n]ineteenth-century explorers commonly requested Africans to sketch maps of unknown areas on the ground. Many of those maps were highly regarded for their accuracy"<sup id="cite_ref-Bassett,_Thomas_J._1994_36-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bassett,_Thomas_J._1994-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> but were not printed in Europe unless Europeans verified them.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Siege_of_Belgrade_(N%C3%A1ndorfeh%C3%A9rv%C3%A1r)_1456.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Siege_of_Belgrade_%28N%C3%A1ndorfeh%C3%A9rv%C3%A1r%29_1456.jpg/170px-Siege_of_Belgrade_%28N%C3%A1ndorfeh%C3%A9rv%C3%A1r%29_1456.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="299" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Siege_of_Belgrade_%28N%C3%A1ndorfeh%C3%A9rv%C3%A1r%29_1456.jpg/255px-Siege_of_Belgrade_%28N%C3%A1ndorfeh%C3%A9rv%C3%A1r%29_1456.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Siege_of_Belgrade_%28N%C3%A1ndorfeh%C3%A9rv%C3%A1r%29_1456.jpg/340px-Siege_of_Belgrade_%28N%C3%A1ndorfeh%C3%A9rv%C3%A1r%29_1456.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1791" data-file-height="3147" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Siege_of_Belgrade_(N%C3%A1ndorfeh%C3%A9rv%C3%A1r)_1456.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ottoman_wars_in_Europe" title="Ottoman wars in Europe">Ottoman wars in Europe</a></div></div></div>
<p>Imperialism in ancient times is clear in the history of China and in the history of western Asia and the Mediterranean—an unending succession of empires. The tyrannical empire of the Assyrians was replaced (6th–4th century BCE) by that of the Persians, in strong contrast to the Assyrian in its liberal treatment of subjected peoples, assuring it long duration. It eventually gave way to the imperialism of Greece. When Greek imperialism reached an apex under Alexander the Great (356–323 BCE), a union of the eastern Mediterranean with western Asia was achieved. But the cosmopolis, in which all citizens of the world would live harmoniously together in equality, remained a dream of Alexander. It was partially realized when the Romans built their empire from Britain to Egypt.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Cultural_imperialism" title="Cultural imperialism">Cultural imperialism</a> is an extremely fuzzy concept, pointing to the supposed influence of one dominant culture over others, i.e. a form of <a href="/wiki/Soft_power" title="Soft power">soft power</a>, which changes the moral, cultural, and societal <a href="/wiki/Worldview" class="mw-redirect" title="Worldview">worldview</a> of the subordinate country. In some ways, this is such an expansion of the concept of imperialism as to be meaningless. This is more than just "foreign" music, television or film becoming popular with young people, but that popular culture changing their own expectations of life and their desire for their own country to become more like the foreign country depicted. For example, depictions of opulent American lifestyles in the soap opera <a href="/wiki/Dallas_(1978_TV_series)#Dallas_and_the_Cold_War" title="Dallas (1978 TV series)">Dallas during the Cold War</a> changed the expectations of Romanians; a more recent example is the influence of smuggled <a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Korean</a> drama series in <a href="/wiki/North_Korea" title="North Korea">North Korea</a>. The importance of soft power is not lost on authoritarian regimes, fighting such influence with bans on foreign popular culture, control of the internet and unauthorised satellite dishes etc. Nor is such a usage of culture recent, as part of Roman imperialism local elites would be exposed to the benefits and luxuries of Roman culture and lifestyle, with the aim that they would then become willing participants.
</p><p>Imperialism has been subject to moral or immoral censure by its critics<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Avoid_weasel_words" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:Avoid weasel words"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too vague attribution or weasel words. (December 2014)">which?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>, and thus the term is frequently used in international propaganda as a pejorative for expansionist and aggressive foreign policy.<sup id="cite_ref-IESS_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IESS-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Age_of_Imperialism">Age of Imperialism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Imperialism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Age of Imperialism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/International_relations,_1648%E2%80%931814" title="International relations, 1648–1814">International relations, 1648–1814</a> and <a href="/wiki/International_relations_of_the_Great_Powers_(1814%E2%80%931919)" title="International relations of the Great Powers (1814–1919)">International relations of the Great Powers (1814–1919)</a></div>
<p>The Age of Imperialism, a time period beginning around 1760, saw European industrializing nations, engaging in the process of colonizing, influencing, and annexing other parts of the world.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>  19th century episodes included  the "<a href="/wiki/Scramble_for_Africa" title="Scramble for Africa">Scramble for Africa</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Colonial_Africa_1913_map.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Colonial_Africa_1913_map.svg/290px-Colonial_Africa_1913_map.svg.png" decoding="async" width="290" height="270" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Colonial_Africa_1913_map.svg/435px-Colonial_Africa_1913_map.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Colonial_Africa_1913_map.svg/580px-Colonial_Africa_1913_map.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1390" data-file-height="1295" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Colonial_Africa_1913_map.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Scramble_for_Africa" title="Scramble for Africa">Africa</a>, divided into colonies under multiple European empires, c. 1913 <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #f7fab2; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Belgium</div><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #bbfdd9; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Germany</div><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #eaaff7; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Spain</div><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #b6e3fc; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;France</div><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #fbc5c0; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Great Britain</div><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #d2f89b; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Italy</div><div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #c0a6f2; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;Portugal</div></div></div></div>
<p>In the 1970s British historians <a href="/wiki/John_Andrew_Gallagher" class="mw-redirect" title="John Andrew Gallagher">John Gallagher</a> (1919–1980) and <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Robinson" title="Ronald Robinson">Ronald Robinson</a> (1920–1999) argued that European leaders rejected the notion that "imperialism" required formal, legal control by one government over a colonial region. Much more important was informal control of independent areas.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> According to Wm. Roger Louis, "In their view, historians have been mesmerized by formal empire and maps of the world with regions colored red. The bulk of British emigration, trade, and capital went to areas outside the formal British Empire. Key to their thinking is the idea of empire 'informally if possible and formally if necessary.'"<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>  Oron Hale says that Gallagher and Robinson looked at the British involvement in Africa where they "found few capitalists, less capital, and not much pressure from the alleged traditional promoters of colonial expansion.  Cabinet decisions to annex or not to annex were made, usually on the basis of political or geopolitical considerations."<sup id="cite_ref-Hale_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hale-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>6</span></sup>
</p><p>Looking at the main empires from 1875–1914, historians estimate a mixed record in terms of profitability.  At first planners expected that colonies would provide an excellent captive market for manufactured items.  Apart from the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a>, this was seldom true.  By the 1890s, imperialists saw the economic benefit primarily in the production of inexpensive raw materials to feed the domestic manufacturing sector.  Overall, Great Britain did very well in terms of profits from India, especially <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Bengal" class="mw-redirect" title="Mughal Bengal">Mughal Bengal</a>, but not from most of the rest of its empire. The Netherlands did very well in the East Indies. Germany and Italy got very little trade or raw materials from their empires. France did slightly better. The Belgian Congo was notoriously profitable when it was a capitalistic rubber plantation owned and operated by King Leopold II as a private enterprise.  However, scandal after scandal regarding very badly mistreated labour led the international community to force the government of Belgium to take it over in 1908, and it became much less profitable.  The Philippines cost the United States much more than expected because of military action against rebels.<sup id="cite_ref-Hale_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hale-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>7–10</span></sup>
</p><p>Because of the resources made available by imperialism, the world's economy grew significantly and became much more interconnected in the decades before <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, making the many imperial powers rich and prosperous.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Europe's expansion into territorial imperialism was largely focused on economic growth by collecting resources from colonies, in combination with assuming political control by military and political means. The colonization of India in the mid-18th century offers an example of this focus: there, the "British exploited the political weakness of the <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Empire" title="Mughal Empire">Mughal</a> state, and, while military activity was important at various times, the economic and administrative incorporation of local elites was also of crucial significance" for the establishment of control over the subcontinent's resources, markets, and manpower.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> Although a substantial number of colonies had been designed to provide economic profit and to ship resources to home ports in the 17th and 18th centuries, Fieldhouse<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (January 2018)">who?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> suggests that in the 19th and 20th centuries in places such as Africa and Asia, this idea is not necessarily valid:<sup id="cite_ref-Painter_&amp;_Jeffrey_14-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Painter_&amp;_Jeffrey-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>183–84</span></sup>
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Modern empires were not artificially constructed economic machines. The second expansion of Europe was a complex historical process in which political, social and emotional forces in Europe and on the periphery were more influential than calculated imperialism. Individual colonies might serve an economic purpose; collectively no empire had any definable function, economic or otherwise. Empires represented only a particular phase in the ever-changing relationship of Europe with the rest of the world: analogies with industrial systems or investment in real estate were simply misleading.<sup id="cite_ref-Painter_&amp;_Jeffrey_14-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Painter_&amp;_Jeffrey-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>184</span></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>During this time, European merchants had the ability to "roam the high seas and appropriate surpluses from around the world (sometimes peaceably, sometimes violently) and to concentrate them in Europe".<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:British_ships_in_Canton.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/British_ships_in_Canton.jpg/220px-British_ships_in_Canton.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="127" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/British_ships_in_Canton.jpg/330px-British_ships_in_Canton.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/British_ships_in_Canton.jpg/440px-British_ships_in_Canton.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1383" data-file-height="800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:British_ships_in_Canton.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>British assault on <a href="/wiki/Guangzhou" title="Guangzhou">Canton</a> during the <a href="/wiki/First_Opium_War" title="First Opium War">First Opium War</a>, May 1841</div></div></div>
<p>European expansion greatly accelerated in the 19th century. To obtain raw materials, Europe expanded imports from other countries and from the colonies. European industrialists sought raw materials such as dyes, cotton, vegetable oils, and metal ores from overseas. Concurrently, industrialization was quickly making Europe the center of manufacturing and economic growth, driving resource needs.<sup id="cite_ref-Adas_2008_54-58_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Adas_2008_54-58-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Communication became much more advanced during European expansion. With the invention of railroads and telegraphs, it became easier to communicate with other countries and to extend the administrative control of a home nation over its colonies. Steam railroads and steam-driven ocean shipping made possible the fast, cheap transport of massive amounts of goods to and from colonies.<sup id="cite_ref-Adas_2008_54-58_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Adas_2008_54-58-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Along with advancements in communication, Europe also continued to advance in military technology. European chemists made new explosives that made artillery much more deadly.  By the 1880s, the <a href="/wiki/Machine_gun" title="Machine gun">machine gun</a> had become a reliable battlefield weapon. This technology gave European armies an advantage over their opponents, as armies in less-developed countries were still fighting with arrows, swords, and leather shields (e.g. the Zulus in Southern Africa during the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Zulu_War" title="Anglo-Zulu War">Anglo-Zulu War</a> of 1879).<sup id="cite_ref-Adas_2008_54-58_46-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Adas_2008_54-58-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>. Some exceptions of armies that managed to get nearly on par with the European expeditions and standards include the Ethiopian armies at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Adwa" title="Battle of Adwa">Battle of Adwa</a>, the Chinese <a href="/wiki/Ever_Victorious_Army" title="Ever Victorious Army">Ever Victorious Army</a> and the Japanese <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Army_of_Japan" class="mw-redirect" title="Imperial Army of Japan">Imperial Army of Japan</a>, but these still relied heavily on weapon imports from Europe and often on European military advisors and adventurers.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Theories_of_imperialism">Theories of imperialism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Imperialism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Theories of imperialism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Anglophone academic studies often base their theories regarding imperialism on the British experience of Empire. The term <i>imperialism</i> was originally introduced into English in its present sense in the late 1870s by opponents of the allegedly aggressive and ostentatious imperial policies of <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Prime Minister of the United Kingdom">British Prime Minister</a> <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Disraeli" title="Benjamin Disraeli">Benjamin Disraeli</a>. Supporters of "imperialism" such as <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Chamberlain" title="Joseph Chamberlain">Joseph Chamberlain</a> quickly appropriated the concept. For some,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (September 2016)">who?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> imperialism designated a policy of idealism and philanthropy; others alleged that it was characterized by political self-interest, and a growing number associated it with capitalist greed.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/John_A._Hobson" class="mw-redirect" title="John A. Hobson">John A. Hobson</a>, A leading English Liberal, developed a highly influential interpretation of <i>Imperialism: A Study</i> (1902) that expanded on his belief that free enterprise capitalism had a negative impact on the majority of the population.  In <i>Imperialism</i> he argued that the financing of overseas empires drained money that was needed at home.  It was invested abroad because lower wages paid the workers overseas made for higher profits and higher rates of return, compared to domestic wages.  So although domestic wages remained higher, they did not grow nearly as fast as they might have otherwise.  Exporting capital, he concluded, put a lid on the growth of domestic wages in the domestic standard of living. .  By the 1970s, historians such as David K. Fieldhouse<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> and Oron Hale could argue that "the Hobsonian foundation has been almost completely demolished."<sup id="cite_ref-Hale_42-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hale-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>5–6</span></sup>  The British experience failed to support it.  However, European Socialists picked up Hobson's ideas and made it into their own theory of imperialism, most notably in Lenin's <i>Imperialism, the Highest Stage of Capitalism</i> (1916). Lenin portrayed <a href="/wiki/Theory_of_Imperialism" class="mw-redirect" title="Theory of Imperialism">Imperialism as the closure of the world market and the end of capitalist free-competition</a> that arose from the need for capitalist economies to constantly expand investment, material resources and manpower in such a way that necessitated colonial expansion. Later Marxist theoreticians echo this conception of imperialism as a structural feature of capitalism, which explained the World War as the battle between imperialists for control of external markets.  Lenin's treatise became a standard textbook that flourished until the collapse of communism in 1989–91.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Some theoreticians on the non-Communist left have emphasized the structural or systemic character of "imperialism". Such writers have expanded the period associated with the term so that it now designates neither a policy, nor a short space of decades in the late 19th century, but a world system extending over a period of centuries, often going back to <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Columbus" title="Christopher Columbus">Christopher Columbus</a> and, in some accounts, to the <a href="/wiki/Crusades" title="Crusades">Crusades</a>. As the application of the term has expanded, its meaning has shifted along five distinct but often parallel axes: the moral, the economic, the systemic, the cultural, and the temporal. Those changes reflect—among other shifts in sensibility—a growing unease, even great distaste, with the pervasiveness of such power, specifically, Western power.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Historians and political theorists have long debated the correlation between capitalism, class and imperialism. Much of the debate was pioneered by such theorists as J. A. Hobson (1858–1940), <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Schumpeter" title="Joseph Schumpeter">Joseph Schumpeter</a> (1883–1950), <a href="/wiki/Thorstein_Veblen" title="Thorstein Veblen">Thorstein Veblen</a> (1857–1929), and <a href="/wiki/Norman_Angell" title="Norman Angell">Norman Angell</a> (1872–1967). While these non-Marxist writers were at their most prolific before World War I, they remained active in the <a href="/wiki/Interwar_period" title="Interwar period">interwar years</a>. Their combined work informed the study of imperialism and its impact on Europe, as well as contributing to reflections on the rise of the military-political complex in the United States from the 1950s. Hobson argued that domestic social reforms could cure the international disease of imperialism by removing its economic foundation. Hobson theorized that state intervention through taxation could boost broader consumption, create wealth, and encourage a peaceful, tolerant, multipolar world order.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Walter_Rodney" title="Walter Rodney">Walter Rodney</a>, in his 1972 classic <a href="/wiki/How_Europe_Underdeveloped_Africa" title="How Europe Underdeveloped Africa">How Europe Underdeveloped Africa</a>, proposes the idea that imperialism is a phase of capitalism "in which Western European capitalist countries, the US, and Japan established political, economic, military and cultural hegemony over other parts of the world which were initially at a lower level and therefore could not resist domination."<sup id="cite_ref-:4_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> As a result, Imperialism "for many years embraced the whole world – one part being the exploiters and the other the exploited, one part being dominated and the other acting as overlords, one part making policy and the other being dependent."<sup id="cite_ref-:4_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_determinism">Environmental determinism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Imperialism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Environmental determinism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The concept of <a href="/wiki/Environmental_determinism" title="Environmental determinism">environmental determinism</a> served as a moral justification for the domination of certain territories and peoples. The environmental determinist school of thought held that the environment in which certain people lived determined those persons' behaviours; and this validated their domination. For example, the <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">Western world</a> saw people living in tropical environments as "less civilized", therefore justifying colonial control as a <a href="/wiki/Civilizing_mission" title="Civilizing mission">civilizing mission</a>. Across the three major waves of <a href="/wiki/European_colonialism" class="mw-redirect" title="European colonialism">European colonialism</a> (the first in the Americas, the second in Asia and the last in Africa), <a href="/wiki/Environmental_determinism" title="Environmental determinism">environmental determinism</a> served to place categorically indigenous people in a racial hierarchy. This takes two forms, <a href="/wiki/Orientalism" title="Orientalism">orientalism</a> and tropicality.
</p><p>Some geographic scholars under colonizing empires divided the world into <a href="/wiki/Climate_zone" class="mw-redirect" title="Climate zone">climatic zones</a>. These scholars believed that Northern Europe and the Mid-Atlantic <a href="/wiki/Temperate_climate" title="Temperate climate">temperate climate</a> produced a hard-working, moral, and upstanding human being. In contrast, tropical climates allegedly yielded lazy attitudes, sexual promiscuity, exotic culture, and moral degeneracy. The people of these climates were believed to be in need of guidance and intervention from a European empire to aid in the governing of a more evolved social structure; they were seen as incapable of such a feat. Similarly, orientalism could promote a view of a people based on their geographical location.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Imperialism_by_country">Imperialism by country</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Imperialism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Imperialism by country">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Britain">Britain</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Imperialism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Britain">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Historiography_of_the_British_Empire" title="Historiography of the British Empire">Historiography of the British Empire</a> and <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tipu_Sultan,_Indian_warrior_Emperor_of_Mysore.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Tipu_Sultan%2C_Indian_warrior_Emperor_of_Mysore.gif/170px-Tipu_Sultan%2C_Indian_warrior_Emperor_of_Mysore.gif" decoding="async" width="170" height="239" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Tipu_Sultan%2C_Indian_warrior_Emperor_of_Mysore.gif/255px-Tipu_Sultan%2C_Indian_warrior_Emperor_of_Mysore.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Tipu_Sultan%2C_Indian_warrior_Emperor_of_Mysore.gif/340px-Tipu_Sultan%2C_Indian_warrior_Emperor_of_Mysore.gif 2x" data-file-width="648" data-file-height="912" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tipu_Sultan,_Indian_warrior_Emperor_of_Mysore.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Anglophobe and modern India's first freedom fighter <a href="/wiki/Tipu_Sultan" title="Tipu Sultan">Tipu Sultan</a>, friend of <a href="/wiki/Napoleone_Bonaparte" class="mw-redirect" title="Napoleone Bonaparte">Napoleone Bonaparte</a>, confronts the <a href="/wiki/British_people" title="British people">British</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Seringapatam_(1792)" title="Siege of Seringapatam (1792)">Siege of Srirangapatna</a>, where he is later assassinated.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Resa_del_bacino_del_Brandewater.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Resa_del_bacino_del_Brandewater.jpg/220px-Resa_del_bacino_del_Brandewater.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="151" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Resa_del_bacino_del_Brandewater.jpg/330px-Resa_del_bacino_del_Brandewater.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Resa_del_bacino_del_Brandewater.jpg/440px-Resa_del_bacino_del_Brandewater.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="412" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Resa_del_bacino_del_Brandewater.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The end result of the <a href="/wiki/Boer_Wars" class="mw-redirect" title="Boer Wars">Boer Wars</a> was the annexation of the <a href="/wiki/Boer_Republics" title="Boer Republics">Boer Republics</a> to the British Empire in 1902</div></div></div>
<p>Britain's imperialist ambitions can be seen as early as the 16th century as the <a href="/wiki/Tudor_conquest_of_Ireland" title="Tudor conquest of Ireland">Tudor conquest of Ireland</a> began in the 1530s. In 1599 the British <a href="/wiki/East_India_Company" title="East India Company">East India Company</a> was established and was chartered by Queen Elizabeth in the following year.<sup id="cite_ref-Painter_&amp;_Jeffrey_14-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Painter_&amp;_Jeffrey-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>174</span></sup> With the establishment of trading posts in India, the British were able to maintain strength relative to other empires such as the Portuguese who already had set up trading posts in India.<sup id="cite_ref-Painter_&amp;_Jeffrey_14-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Painter_&amp;_Jeffrey-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>174</span></sup> In 1767, the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Mysore_Wars" title="Anglo-Mysore Wars">Anglo-Mysore Wars</a> and other political activity caused exploitation of the East India Company causing the plundering of the local economy, almost bringing the company into bankruptcy.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceC_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceC-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> By the year 1670 Britain's imperialist ambitions were well off as she had colonies in Virginia, Massachusetts, Bermuda, <a href="/wiki/Honduras" title="Honduras">Honduras</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antigua" title="Antigua">Antigua</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barbados" title="Barbados">Barbados</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jamaica" title="Jamaica">Jamaica</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nova_Scotia" title="Nova Scotia">Nova Scotia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceC_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceC-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Due to the vast imperialist ambitions of European countries, Britain had several clashes with France. This competition was evident in the colonization of what is now known as <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>. <a href="/wiki/John_Cabot" title="John Cabot">John Cabot</a> claimed Newfoundland for the British while the French established colonies along the St. Lawrence River and claiming it as "New France".<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Britain continued to expand by colonizing countries such as New Zealand and Australia, both of which were not empty land as they had their own locals and cultures.<sup id="cite_ref-Painter_&amp;_Jeffrey_14-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Painter_&amp;_Jeffrey-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>175</span></sup> Britain's nationalistic movements were evident with the creation of the commonwealth countries where there was a shared nature of national identity.<sup id="cite_ref-Painter_&amp;_Jeffrey_14-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Painter_&amp;_Jeffrey-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>147</span></sup>
</p><p>Following the <a href="/wiki/Proto-industrialization" title="Proto-industrialization">proto-industrialization</a>, the "First" <a href="/wiki/British_Empire#&quot;First&quot;_British_Empire_(1583–1783)" title="British Empire">British Empire</a> was based on <a href="/wiki/Mercantilism" title="Mercantilism">mercantilism</a>, and involved colonies and holdings primarily in North America, the Caribbean, and India. Its growth was reversed by the loss of the American colonies in 1776. Britain made compensating gains in India, Australia, and in constructing an informal economic empire through control of trade and finance in Latin America after the independence of Spanish and Portuguese colonies in about 1820.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>  By the 1840s, Britain had adopted a highly successful policy of <a href="/wiki/Economic_history_of_the_United_Kingdom#19th_century" title="Economic history of the United Kingdom">free trade</a> that gave it dominance in the trade of much of the world.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> After losing its first Empire to the Americans, Britain then turned its attention towards Asia, Africa, and the Pacific. Following the defeat of Napoleonic France in 1815, Britain enjoyed a century of almost unchallenged dominance and expanded its imperial holdings around the globe. <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy#1815–1914" title="Royal Navy">Unchallenged at sea</a>, British dominance was later described as <i><a href="/wiki/Pax_Britannica" title="Pax Britannica">Pax Britannica</a></i> ("British Peace"), a period of relative peace in Europe and the world (1815–1914) during which the British Empire became the global <a href="/wiki/Hegemon" class="mw-redirect" title="Hegemon">hegemon</a> and adopted the role of global policeman. However, this peace was mostly a perceived one from Europe, and the period was still an almost uninterrupted series of colonial wars and disputes. The <a href="/wiki/British_Raj" title="British Raj">British Conquest of India</a>, its intervention against <a href="/wiki/Mehemet_Ali" class="mw-redirect" title="Mehemet Ali">Mehemet Ali</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Burmese_Wars" title="Anglo-Burmese Wars">Anglo-Burmese Wars</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Crimean_War" title="Crimean War">Crimean War</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Opium_Wars" title="Opium Wars">Opium Wars</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Scramble_for_Africa" title="Scramble for Africa">Scramble for Africa</a> to name the most notable conflicts mobilised ample military means to press Britain's lead in the global conquest Europe led across the century.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:212px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Port_Said_from_air.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Port_Said_from_air.jpg/210px-Port_Said_from_air.jpg" decoding="async" width="210" height="154" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Port_Said_from_air.jpg/315px-Port_Said_from_air.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Port_Said_from_air.jpg/420px-Port_Said_from_air.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="588" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Port_Said_from_air.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Smoke rises from oil tanks beside the <a href="/wiki/Suez_Canal" title="Suez Canal">Suez Canal</a> hit during the initial <a href="/wiki/Suez_Crisis" title="Suez Crisis">Anglo-French assault</a> on <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>, 5 November 1956</div></div></div>
<p>In the early 19th century, the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a> began to transform Britain; by the time of <a href="/wiki/The_Great_Exhibition" class="mw-redirect" title="The Great Exhibition">the Great Exhibition</a> in 1851 the country was described as the "workshop of the world".<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> The British Empire expanded to include <a href="/wiki/British_Raj" title="British Raj">India</a>, large <a href="/wiki/British_Empire#Cape_to_Cairo" title="British Empire">parts of Africa</a> and many other territories throughout the world. Alongside the formal control it exerted over its own colonies, British dominance of much of world trade meant that it effectively <a href="/wiki/Informal_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Informal Empire">controlled the economies of many regions</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Latin_America" title="Latin America">Latin America</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> Domestically, political attitudes favoured free trade and laissez-faire policies and a gradual widening of the voting franchise. During this century, the population increased at a dramatic rate, accompanied by rapid urbanisation, causing significant social and economic stresses.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> To seek new markets and sources of raw materials, the <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservative Party</a> under <a href="/wiki/Disraeli" class="mw-redirect" title="Disraeli">Disraeli</a> launched a period of imperialist expansion in Egypt, South Africa, and elsewhere. Canada, Australia, and New Zealand became self-governing dominions.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A resurgence came in the late 19th century with the <a href="/wiki/Scramble_for_Africa" title="Scramble for Africa">Scramble for Africa</a> and major additions in Asia and the Middle East. The British spirit of imperialism was expressed by <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Chamberlain" title="Joseph Chamberlain">Joseph Chamberlain</a> and <a href="/wiki/Archibald_Primrose,_5th_Earl_of_Rosebery" title="Archibald Primrose, 5th Earl of Rosebery">Lord Rosebury</a>, and implemented in Africa by <a href="/wiki/Cecil_Rhodes" title="Cecil Rhodes">Cecil Rhodes</a>. The pseudo-sciences of Social Darwinism and theories of race formed an ideological underpinning and legitimation during this time. Other influential spokesmen included <a href="/wiki/Evelyn_Baring,_1st_Earl_of_Cromer" title="Evelyn Baring, 1st Earl of Cromer">Lord Cromer</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_Curzon,_1st_Marquess_Curzon_of_Kedleston" title="George Curzon, 1st Marquess Curzon of Kedleston">Lord Curzon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Kitchener,_1st_Earl_Kitchener" title="Herbert Kitchener, 1st Earl Kitchener">General Kitchener</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Milner,_1st_Viscount_Milner" title="Alfred Milner, 1st Viscount Milner">Lord Milner</a>, and the writer <a href="/wiki/Rudyard_Kipling" title="Rudyard Kipling">Rudyard Kipling</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> The British Empire was the largest Empire that the world has ever seen both in terms of landmass and population. Its power, both military and economic, remained unmatched for a few decades. After the <a href="/wiki/First_Boer_War" title="First Boer War">First Boer War</a>, the <a href="/wiki/South_African_Republic" title="South African Republic">South African Republic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Orange_Free_State" title="Orange Free State">Orange Free State</a> were recognised by Britain but eventually re-annexed after the <a href="/wiki/Second_Boer_War" title="Second Boer War">Second Boer War</a>. But British power was fading, as the reunited <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">German state founded by the Kingdom of Prussia</a> posed a growing threat to Britain's dominance. As of 1913, Britain was the world's fourth economy, behind the U.S, Russia and Germany. 
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Irish_War_of_Independence" title="Irish War of Independence">Irish War of Independence</a> in 1919-1921 led to the сreation of the Irish Free State. But Britain gained control of former German and Ottoman colonies with the <a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations_mandate" title="League of Nations mandate">League of Nations mandate</a>. Britain now had a practically continuous line of controlled territories from Egypt to Burma and another one from Cairo to Cape Town. However, this period was also the one of the emergence of independence movements based on nationalism and new experiences the colonists had gained in the war.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> decisively weakened Britain's position in the world, especially financially. <a href="/wiki/Decolonization" title="Decolonization">Decolonization</a> movements arose nearly everywhere in the Empire, resulting in <a href="/wiki/Indian_independence_movement" title="Indian independence movement">Indian independence and partition</a> in 1947 and the establishment of independent states in the 1950s. British imperialism showed its frailty in Egypt during the <a href="/wiki/Suez_Crisis" title="Suez Crisis">Suez Crisis</a> in 1956. However, with the United States and Soviet Union emerging from World War II as the sole <a href="/wiki/Superpower" title="Superpower">superpowers</a>, Britain's role as a worldwide power declined significantly and rapidly.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="China">China</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Imperialism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: China">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Chinese_imperialism" title="Chinese imperialism">Chinese imperialism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Qing_dynasty_in_Inner_Asia" title="Qing dynasty in Inner Asia">Qing dynasty in Inner Asia</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:EN-QIN260BCE.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/EN-QIN260BCE.jpg/220px-EN-QIN260BCE.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="202" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/EN-QIN260BCE.jpg/330px-EN-QIN260BCE.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/EN-QIN260BCE.jpg/440px-EN-QIN260BCE.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4724" data-file-height="4348" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:EN-QIN260BCE.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of the Growth of China under Qin Dynasty</div></div></div>
<p>Ancient China was one of the world's oldest empires. Due to its long history of imperialist expansion, China has been seen by its neighboring countries as a threat due to its large population, giant economy, large military force as well as its territorial evolution throughout history.  Starting with the unification of China under the <a href="/wiki/Qin_dynasty" title="Qin dynasty">Qin dynasty</a>, later Chinese dynasties continued to follow its form of expansions.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> 
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:452px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Qing_Empire_circa_1820_EN.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Qing_Empire_circa_1820_EN.svg/450px-Qing_Empire_circa_1820_EN.svg.png" decoding="async" width="450" height="393" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Qing_Empire_circa_1820_EN.svg/675px-Qing_Empire_circa_1820_EN.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Qing_Empire_circa_1820_EN.svg/900px-Qing_Empire_circa_1820_EN.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1172" data-file-height="1024" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Qing_Empire_circa_1820_EN.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Qing Empire ca. 1820, marked the time when the Qing began to rule these areas.</div></div></div>
<p><br />
The most successful Chinese imperial dynasties are <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang</a> and <a href="/wiki/Qing_dynasty" title="Qing dynasty">Qing dynasty</a>, due to its expansions. According to historian Eric Setzekorn examining the 1850-1877 period, China's imperialism was brutal, and "resulted in the deaths of millions....[ Chinese leaders] radically shifted the ethnic balance in favor of Han colonists. This was accomplished through the mass expulsion of ethnic communities and, more directly, the killing of unwanted minority groups, i.e. ethnic cleansing."<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="France">France</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Imperialism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: France">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/French_colonial_empire" title="French colonial empire">French colonial empire</a></div>
